  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1704745475" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1704745475" />
    <a title="Fionnuala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala"><img alt="Fionnuala" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368182051p2/5498525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1704745475?book_show_action=true">Jul 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fionnuala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fionnuala" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala">Fionnuala</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007247189" href="/book/show/984083.Best_of_Myles">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=flannobrien">flannobrien</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=review-may-contain-comic-content">review-may-contain-comic-content</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1704745475" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer884342966750917048"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59647.The_Best_of_Myles' title='The Best of Myles by Myles na gCopaleen' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Best of Myles</a> is a selection from the newspaper articles of Flann O’Brien, author of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/97333.At_Swim_Two_Birds' title='At Swim-Two-Birds by Flann O'Brien' rel='nofollow noopener'>At Swim-Two-Birds</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27208.The_Third_Policeman' title='The Third Policeman by Flann O'Brien' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Third Policeman</a>. The pieces, all for <i>The Irish Times</i>, were written initially under the pseudonym An Broc which means the badger. The name was then changed to An Cruiskeen Lawn, which means the full little jug, but was soon switched to Myles na gCopaleen and then to the definitive version, Myles na Gopaleen.<br/>(It seems the reviewer has been reading Wikipedia -FO'B)<br/><br/>Erm...the name Myles </span>
  <span id="freeText884342966750917048" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59647.The_Best_of_Myles" title="The Best of Myles by Myles na gCopaleen" rel="nofollow noopener">The Best of Myles</a> is a selection from the newspaper articles of Flann O’Brien, author of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/97333.At_Swim_Two_Birds" title="At Swim-Two-Birds by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">At Swim-Two-Birds</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27208.The_Third_Policeman" title="The Third Policeman by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">The Third Policeman</a>. The pieces, all for <i>The Irish Times</i>, were written initially under the pseudonym An Broc which means the badger. The name was then changed to An Cruiskeen Lawn, which means the full little jug, but was soon switched to Myles na gCopaleen and then to the definitive version, Myles na Gopaleen.<br />(It seems the reviewer has been reading Wikipedia -FO'B)<br /><br />Erm...the name Myles na Gopaleen means ‘Myles of the little horses’.<br />(The correct translation is ‘Myles of the ponies’; the principality of the pony should never be subjugated to the imperialism of the horse - FO’B). <br />Flann O’Brien was against imperialism on principle, needless to say. <br />(Why say it in that case? And if there’s any whiskey in the case, I’ll have some of it -FO’B). <br /><br />The..the articles appeared regularly from the early 1940s until shortly before Mr O'Brien, who must surely qualify as Ireland's greatest comic genius, d-died on April 1st 1966.<br />(Just say I died on April Fool’s Day and be done with it -FO’B).<br /><br />The n..name Myles na Gopaleen was inspired by a character in Dion Boucicault’s play, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27196342.The_Colleen_Bawn__Or__the_Brides_of_Garryowen__a_Domestic_Drama_in_Three_Acts___Scholar_s_Choice_Edition" title="The Colleen Bawn; Or, the Brides of Garryowen. a Domestic Drama in Three Acts - Scholar's Choice Edition by Dion Boucicault" rel="nofollow noopener">The Colleen Bawn</a>, which dates from 1860. In Boucicault's melodrama, aimed at American audiences, Myles was a lovelorn Irish peasant who..who distilled alcohol in his spare time.<br />(No half-measures, please, Myles was a full-time poteen maker and drinker - FO’B).<br /> <br />Another of Boucicault’s plays had inspired O’Brien’s compatriot James Joyce to borrow the character Shaun from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17000637.Arrah_Na_Pogue" title="Arrah Na Pogue by Dion Boucicault" rel="nofollow noopener">Arrah Na Pogue</a>, written in 1864. Shaun became a central figure in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11013.Finnegans_Wake" title="Finnegans Wake by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Finnegans Wake</a>, which incidentally was published in 1939, the same year as O’Brien’s first novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/97333.At_Swim_Two_Birds" title="At Swim-Two-Birds by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">At Swim-Two-Birds</a>.<br /> (Do you have to underline that I will be forever eclipsed by Joyce? - FO’B).<br /><br />While the first few pieces Flann O'Brien contributed to <i>The Irish Times</i> were written in the Irish language, the rest were written completely in English. <br />(That is not accurate: the columns frequently reverted to Irish, eg, the 'Corkadorka' articles were written in Irish and several sections of 'The Brother' series were written in a version of Irish transliterated for English speakers, eg, ‘bee kuramack lay the hell’ which means, 'be more careful please'. Another set of articles, 'Literally from the Irish', though in English they were written, an Irish syntax they were having - FO’B)<br /><br />The..the topics covered in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59647.The_Best_of_Myles" title="The Best of Myles by Myles na gCopaleen" rel="nofollow noopener">The Best of Myles</a> range from steam trains to music, from the shortage of alcohol during the war to the abundance of cliché during the same period.<br /> (What is a list of things always said to do? Range. What is always the opposite of a shortage? An abundance -FO'B (groaning)). <br /><br />S...since Mr O’Brien’s two best known novels, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/97333.At_Swim_Two_Birds" title="At Swim-Two-Birds by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">At Swim-Two-Birds</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27208.The_Third_Policeman" title="The Third Policeman by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">The Third Policeman</a> were completed before he began writing for <i>The Irish Times</i>, many critics believe that..that directing his energies towards filling a daily newspaper column with ever more witty and creative material prevented the author from fulfilling his destiny as the leading Irish novelist of the twentieth century. <br />(What action must always be taken in relation to our energies? They must be directed. What must a destiny always be? Fulfilled. I begin to suspect this reviewer hasn’t read the book. If she had, she’d have learned from the ‘The Catechism of Cliché’ section to avoid such worn-out phrases - FO’B).<br /><br />Hmm...while <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/97333.At_Swim_Two_Birds" title="At Swim-Two-Birds by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">At Swim-Two-Birds</a> was greeted with acclaim (groan -FO’B), a fire in the warehouse where the copies were stored meant that it didn’t reach a very wide audience. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27208.The_Third_Policeman" title="The Third Policeman by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">The Third Policeman</a> was <s>similarly ill-fated</s> also unlucky: it failed to find a publisher and the manuscript was <s>subsequently mislaid</s> lost. It didn’t surface again until after Mr O’Brien’s death.<br /> (It was not lost, it was simply lying in the back of a drawer. And before you say anything smart, and I feel you are working up to it, I’m entitled, as the author of 'The Catechism of Cliché', to create a clichéd destiny for my own manuscript if I choose -FO’B).<br /><br /> However, some of the ideas and scenarios from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27208.The_Third_Policeman" title="The Third Policeman by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">The Third Policeman</a> apparently made their way from the back of the drawer into a later novel called <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59642.The_Dalkey_Archive" title="The Dalkey Archive by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">Dalkey Archive</a> which was published in 1964. Incidentally, James Joyce is a character in that novel - after forging his own obituary to avoid being drafted into the army in 1939, he turns up in a village on the outskirts of Dublin where, under an assumed name, he works as a barman. <br />(The reviewer has been reading Wikipedia again I see -FO’B).<br /><br />Phew...I think there is nothing more to be said except that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59647.The_Best_of_Myles" title="The Best of Myles by Myles na gCopaleen" rel="nofollow noopener">The Best of Myles</a> is only one of the selections from Flann O’ Brian's journalistic career that now exist. The following titles are also available:<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/984098.Myles_Before_Myles" title="Myles Before Myles by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">Myles Before Myles</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/984102.Myles_Away_From_Dublin" title="Myles Away From Dublin by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">Myles Away From Dublin</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/984114.The_Hair_Of_The_Dogma_A_Further_Selection_From__Cruiskeen_Lawn_" title="The Hair Of The Dogma A Further Selection From 'Cruiskeen Lawn' by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">The Hair Of The Dogma</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/421395.Further_Cuttings_From_Cruiskeen_Lawn" title="Further Cuttings From Cruiskeen Lawn by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">Further Cuttings From Cruiskeen Lawn</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/899551.At_War" title="At War by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">Flann At War</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20895402.The_Various_Lives_of_Keats_and_Chapman" title="The Various Lives of Keats and Chapman by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">The Various Lives of Keats and Chapman</a><br />(Someone is surely buying a lot of pints with the royalties from all these books and it certainly isn't me -FO’B. Furthermore, what completely non-existent thing is invariably stated to be there? The nothing more that often remains to be said. <br />Ok, in what direction should I now shut? Up. <br />FO’B).</span>
  <a data-text-id="884342966750917048" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

<span id="freeTextContainer12591857473307381713">This bumper-sized collection of Cruiskeen Lawn columns runs to 400 pages in a small 10pt font. You would be mistaken for thinking this covers his entire career at the <i>Irish Times</i>. In fact, it only covers from 1940-1945. Begad!<br/><br/>Bearing this in mind, his output was extraordinary. The range of wit, erudition, linguistic skill and creativity is outrageous. Among the funniest columns are the "Research Bereau," "The Brother" and the "Catechism of Cliché." O'Brien is at his finest when taking a ridiculo</span>
  <span id="freeText12591857473307381713" style="display:none">This bumper-sized collection of Cruiskeen Lawn columns runs to 400 pages in a small 10pt font. You would be mistaken for thinking this covers his entire career at the <i>Irish Times</i>. In fact, it only covers from 1940-1945. Begad!<br /><br />Bearing this in mind, his output was extraordinary. The range of wit, erudition, linguistic skill and creativity is outrageous. Among the funniest columns are the "Research Bereau," "The Brother" and the "Catechism of Cliché." O'Brien is at his finest when taking a ridiculous idea and stretching it to breaking point.<br /><br />His grasp of language is also amazing. This book bedazzles with endless wordplay and puns. There are also frequent forays into Latin and French, as well as an entire section written in Gaelic. The section "Miscellenous" is less successful. There are one too many rambling and baffling columns here, and the book does seem to run on forever.<br /><br />Still: a top read and the definitive collection of O'Brien's articles and genius during wartime Ireland. A pint of Flann is your only man.</span>

<div id="review_3981554" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3981554" />
    <a title="Kay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/241691-kay"><img alt="Kay" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541802339p2/241691.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3981554?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kay" href="/user/show/241691-kay">Kay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/241691-kay?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/241691-kay?shelf=humor-whimsy">humor-whimsy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/241691-kay?shelf=ireland-irish-lit">ireland-irish-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/241691-kay?shelf=satire">satire</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/241691-kay?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3981554" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16670267458242963474">A good deal of this collection was beyond my comprehension -- being firmly rooted in a time and place that I have scant knowledge of. What can you expect of a book that consists of thirty years' work by a newspaper columnist (and novelist), however brilliant? However, even though the average reader isn't privileged to understand all the references Myles makes to local concerns, at times his columns hit a mark of comic brilliance that is unmatched. <br/><br/>Consider, for example, several columns devoted t</span>
  <span id="freeText16670267458242963474" style="display:none">A good deal of this collection was beyond my comprehension -- being firmly rooted in a time and place that I have scant knowledge of. What can you expect of a book that consists of thirty years' work by a newspaper columnist (and novelist), however brilliant? However, even though the average reader isn't privileged to understand all the references Myles makes to local concerns, at times his columns hit a mark of comic brilliance that is unmatched. <br /><br />Consider, for example, several columns devoted to Myles' concept of "Buchhandlung" - perennial favorites of mine. Myles' idea was that most people would rather <i>appear</i> to be well read than to actually <i>be</i> well read. So, in a fine entrepreneurial scheme, he proposes setting up a "Bookhander" service -- he will personally come in and give your library the well-thumbed look that makes it seem you <i>have</i> read all those impressive tomes. "Why should a wealthy person... be put to the trouble of pretending to read at all? Why not a professional book-handler to go in and suitably maul his library for so-much a shelf? Such a person, if properly qualified, could make a fortune." <br /><br />He then goes through a litany of the types of "handling" that he would be willing to do - and how much each type would cost, from "Popular Handling" (including insertion of tram tickets or other casual book marks to dog-earing four pages per volume) to "Premier Handling" (insertion of cryptic learned marginalia, extensive underlining, the insertion of a leaflet in French on the works of Victor Hugo).<br /><br />Miles spins out this idea for an impressive number of pages. And if you can read it without tears of laughter streaming down your face, then there's little hope for you in this world of strife. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16670267458242963474" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_103238377" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/103238377" />
    <a title="Dan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/929972-dan"><img alt="Dan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267461501p2/929972.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/103238377?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan" href="/user/show/929972-dan">Dan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780586089507" href="/book/show/984079.The_Best_of_Myles">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/929972-dan?shelf=journalism">journalism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer103238377" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3810318939120473650">A collection of humorous columns written by Brian O’Nolan for the <a href='http://www.irishtimes.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>Irish Times</i>
</a>, and which for legal reasons appeared in that newspaper under a pseudonym de plume (false name for a French goose quill). “Myles na cGopaleen,” the newspapers call him (pronounced Flann O’Brien, Gaelic orthography being what it is—and “Flann” means “Irish,” don’t you know).<br/><br/>The book is divided into chapters, with O’Brien’s columns grouped together by subject. The first chapter, describing the activities of the WAAMA</span>
  <span id="freeText3810318939120473650" style="display:none">A collection of humorous columns written by Brian O’Nolan for the <a href="http://www.irishtimes.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Irish Times</i>
</a>, and which for legal reasons appeared in that newspaper under a pseudonym de plume (false name for a French goose quill). “Myles na cGopaleen,” the newspapers call him (pronounced Flann O’Brien, Gaelic orthography being what it is—and “Flann” means “Irish,” don’t you know).<br /><br />The book is divided into chapters, with O’Brien’s columns grouped together by subject. The first chapter, describing the activities of the WAAMA (the Irish Writers, Actors, Artists, Musicians Association) is screwball comedy. The type of humor employed in the columns included in the chapter on inventions (representing the work of the Myles na gCopaleen Central Research Bureau) and the chapter on different types of bores is closer to the satire typical of publications like <a href="http://www.dccomics.com/mad/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Mad Magazine</i>
</a>.<br /><br />The subject that gets the greatest amount of attention in O’Brien’s book is that of clichés. This is not entirely surprising: as columnist for the <i>Irish Times</i> (and writer and editor for the student journal <i>Blather</i> at <a href="http://www.ucd.ie/" rel="nofollow noopener">University College</a>--see <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/984098.Myles_Before_Myles__Paladin_Books_" title="Myles Before Myles (Paladin Books) by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">Myles Before Myles</a> for this early work), it is likely O’Brien saw many feints at communication choked by the weeds of their own verbiage (FWIW, in the contemporary moment, with the <a href="http://www.cnn.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">24-hour news cycle</a>, ultra-modern electronic communication devices, and {cliché alert!} “new and improved” forms of invasive advertising, we all enjoy {?} enhanced opportunities of collecting and trading overused phrases that far outstrip anything we dream O’Brien could have dreamed. Or do I dream?)<br /><br />As of this writing, Wikipedia says that "a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clich%C3%A9" rel="nofollow noopener">cliché</a> may sometimes be used in a work of fiction for comedic effect.” In fact, O’Brien wrote a novel employing this technique: in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59641.The_Poor_Mouth_A_Bad_Story_About_the_Hard_Life" title="The Poor Mouth A Bad Story About the Hard Life by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">The Poor Mouth: A Bad Story About the Hard Life</a>, he parodies the language and situations common to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/An_B%C3%A9al_Bocht" rel="nofollow noopener">“Gaeltacht autobiography,”</a> a popular genre of Irish fiction in which is represented the miserable and poverty-stricken life of a protagonist and his or her people. One example of this type of novel is <i>Peg</i>, in which <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/366423.Peig_Sayers" title="Peig Sayers" rel="nofollow noopener">Peig Sayers</a> “recounts her life, especially the latter half, as a series of misfortunes in which much of her family die by disease, drowning or other mishap” (thanks again, Wikipedia).<br /><br />In the newspaper articles included in <i>The Best of Myles</i>, O’Brien employs a number of techniques to make fun of clichés. For instance, in addition to parodying the language of bureaucrats, art critics and lawyers, he deploys clichés ironically, calling attention to them by employing them but setting them off from the rest of the passage in which they appear through the use of parentheses. He includes humorous stories representing absurd situations, the “punch lines” to which are cliches (or puns or spoonerisms constructed from the same), the effect of which is to suggest that only in the context of such fantastic situations could these worn-out phrases become meaningful once again. In one chapter, he makes fun of the cliché by employing the form of the catechism, implying that some clichés have become so rigid as to have become part of ideological dogma. As well, he analyzes passages from speeches and from newspaper articles employing hackneyed phrases and shows how these contribute to imprecision of meaning. It was while reading some of these latter that I had to reach for a copy of the essay <a href="http://www.george-orwell.org/Politics_and_the_English_Language/0.html" rel="nofollow noopener">“Politics and the English Language,”</a> in which <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3706.George_Orwell" title="George Orwell" rel="nofollow noopener">George Orwell</a> comments on “dying metaphors,” “pretentious diction” and “meaningless words,” and argues that the English language “becomes ugly and inaccurate because our thoughts are foolish, but the slovenliness of our language makes it easier for us to have foolish thoughts.” In that essay, the first of Orwell's six “rules” for increased precision in communication reads: “Never use a metaphor, simile or other figure of speech which you are used to seeing in print.” Orwell does not mention it, but I would add that the exception to this rule would be that you could use a metaphor, simile or figure of speech that you are used to seeing in print if you are using it for its entertainment value, as O’Brien does.<br /><br />At this point, I would want to suggest that O’Brien’s employment of cliché as a comic device substantiates <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/455.Marshall_McLuhan" title="Marshall McLuhan" rel="nofollow noopener">Marshall McLuhan</a>’s notion that a medium, once replaced by a newer medium, becomes the content of that newer medium—but that’s a whole other essay that would take me far(ther) off the topic—that being that Flann O’Brien’s <i>The Best of Myles</i> is a funny book (frequently LOL funny) and that it has makes some very good points about language and communication.<br /><br /><i>Et ca suffit</i>. Enough is enough.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3810318939120473650" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText820832898013898436" style="display:none">This sort of book is difficult to rate. Taken individually, the pieces in the book are very funny, witty, intelligent and sometimes surreal. If I was reading Myles piece once a day in the Irish Times with the different formats spread around through the year, then I'd give the content 4 stars, but as a collection the pieces suffer in context. Two or three "Keats &amp; Chapman" sketches or The Brother pieces are amusing, but after 45 pages of Keats and Chapman, it's hard to raise even a wry smile at the puns. So the format of the book takes the star rating down to about 2. So I split the difference and gave it 3.</span>

<div id="review_12738349" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12738349" />
    <a title="Bill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/794396-bill"><img alt="Bill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1200581069p2/794396.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12738349" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13058834717534130938">Besides writing novels under the pseudonym Flann O'Brien, Brian O'Nolan also wrote a regular column for the Irish Times under the name Myles na gCopaleen ("Myles of the Little Horses"). This book collects some of the best ones. They tend to be sarcastic observations on Dublin life in the 1940s-50s, which might seem pretty arcane, but just as you don't have to live in Chicago to appreciate Dave Barry, you don't have to be a Dubliner to enjoy Myles. The persona he creates for himself would rank as</span>
  <span id="freeText13058834717534130938" style="display:none">Besides writing novels under the pseudonym Flann O'Brien, Brian O'Nolan also wrote a regular column for the Irish Times under the name Myles na gCopaleen ("Myles of the Little Horses"). This book collects some of the best ones. They tend to be sarcastic observations on Dublin life in the 1940s-50s, which might seem pretty arcane, but just as you don't have to live in Chicago to appreciate Dave Barry, you don't have to be a Dubliner to enjoy Myles. The persona he creates for himself would rank as one of the great characters of fiction, if O'Nolan had ever put him in a novel.</span>

<div id="review_719150" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/719150" />
    <a title="Bernard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59520-bernard"><img alt="Bernard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509058507p2/59520.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a title="Bernard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bernard" href="/user/show/59520-bernard">Bernard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59520-bernard?shelf=defyingcategorization">defyingcategorization</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer719150" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14161493986471413969">I'm on my second copy of this book - I read and re-read the first copy until it fell apart. O'Brien's daily newspaper column in the Irish Times was an outlet for an under-appreciated intellectual talent. There's everything here from anarchic humour to social satire and dry wit, always flavoured with a hearty contempt for pretension in all its forms.<br/>Humorous prose at its finest. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2384277192" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2384277192" />
    <a title="David Mccarthy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22180047-david-mccarthy"><img alt="David Mccarthy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373755900p2/22180047.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2384277192?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Mccarthy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Mccarthy" href="/user/show/22180047-david-mccarthy">David Mccarthy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2384277192" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17357879614417535459">Brian O'Nolan wrote under the nom de plume Myles na gCopaleen (his other pen name was Flann O'Brien) for the Irish Times, and other news publications, in the mid Twentieth century. His columns consisted largely of comedic pieces and satirical jabs at various sections of Irish society, from wealthy Dublin cultural dilettantes to "the plain people of Ireland", essentially he got paid, to use the vernacular of the isles, to take the piss out of people. In style and substance his work for the Irish </span>
  <span id="freeText17357879614417535459" style="display:none">Brian O'Nolan wrote under the nom de plume Myles na gCopaleen (his other pen name was Flann O'Brien) for the Irish Times, and other news publications, in the mid Twentieth century. His columns consisted largely of comedic pieces and satirical jabs at various sections of Irish society, from wealthy Dublin cultural dilettantes to "the plain people of Ireland", essentially he got paid, to use the vernacular of the isles, to take the piss out of people. In style and substance his work for the Irish Time could be comparable to Mark Twain's stint with San Franciscan journals.<br /><br />My favorite pieces he did was a series of columns advertising his services as a book man-handler for wealthy patrons who wanted show libraries that looked like they were well read without actually going to the bother of actually reading anything. To give you a taste of what to expect in this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17357879614417535459" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3329769569" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16388196227138689846">You’ve got to especially read the stories under the titles The Brother, The Plain People of Ireland, For Steam Men, Keats and Chapman, and The Myles Na Gopaleen Catechism of Cliché. Brilliant, all. Brian O’Nolan, a.k.a. most notably, Flan O’Brian and Myles na Gopaleen, was a comic genius very different in style but equally as funny as P.G. Wodehouse. These are the only two writers for whom I own all of the books they’ve written and enjoy reading biographies about them.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1622533448" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1622533448" />
    <a title="Marco Ocram" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/55303618-marco-ocram"><img alt="Marco Ocram" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1622533448?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marco Ocram" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marco Ocram" href="/user/show/55303618-marco-ocram">Marco Ocram</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1622533448" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6876848666427461958">Mind-blowing. It consists of a few hundred extracts from a daily column in the Irish Times, mainly from the 1940's I would guess, supposedly written by a fictitious character (Myles na gCopaleen)who is an omniscient polymath with authoritative views on every possible subject (it was actually written by Brian O'Nolan, who was better known as his pseudonym Flan O'Brien). The language is dazzling, as are the amazingly inventive subjects. Some of the contemporary references mean absolutely nothing t</span>
  <span id="freeText6876848666427461958" style="display:none">Mind-blowing. It consists of a few hundred extracts from a daily column in the Irish Times, mainly from the 1940's I would guess, supposedly written by a fictitious character (Myles na gCopaleen)who is an omniscient polymath with authoritative views on every possible subject (it was actually written by Brian O'Nolan, who was better known as his pseudonym Flan O'Brien). The language is dazzling, as are the amazingly inventive subjects. Some of the contemporary references mean absolutely nothing to me, and that takes the edge off some of the extracts, but overall it is brilliant. Myles made me realise that fiction is just that- fiction, so as the author make up everything, including your name, your personality, your expertise, your publishing record, the reviews of your books- the lot! <br />You read about authors who spend three months researching the spark-plugs on a jumbo jet so they can write a realistic paragraph about them in Chapter 98 of a thriller- make-it up! If Myles were writing today he'd announce that he was suing Boeing for patent infringements relating to sintered ceramics with tungsten nano-inserts, and that he'd had to develop a special asymmetric magnetic oscillator to 'settle' the nano-particles in the atomic lattice in order to eliminate harmonic stress transients, or even transient stress harmonics, and it would all be made up but it would still all sound convincing because it was written by someone with such an amazing gift for words.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6876848666427461958" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer45548695" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12673539457185785343">The first thing that comes to mind when reading something like this, or the collected Don Marquis, is that I wish there were columnists writing like this today. The closest we've come in my lifetime is probably Dave Barry, and that's not really a great comparison, as Barry's style of humor is very different.<br/><br/>This might have gotten five stars, but I don't like the way they compiled these columns, which they did by theme. Some of the themes feel more forced than others, and they still have 100 page</span>
  <span id="freeText12673539457185785343" style="display:none">The first thing that comes to mind when reading something like this, or the collected Don Marquis, is that I wish there were columnists writing like this today. The closest we've come in my lifetime is probably Dave Barry, and that's not really a great comparison, as Barry's style of humor is very different.<br /><br />This might have gotten five stars, but I don't like the way they compiled these columns, which they did by theme. Some of the themes feel more forced than others, and they still have 100 pages of "miscellaneous" at the end. I love many of his themes, like the Brother, and Keats &amp; Chapman, but by the time you get to the end of their thematic section they are gone never to crop up again, and often they are better in small doses, which would be better suited to splitting them up throughout as I understand O'Brien did.*<br /><br />Still great stuff, but I would love it if they organized it in a more organic fashion, closer to how they originally appeared in print.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />*I'm referring to him as O'Brien because that appears to be the agreed upon pseudonym under which to sort all of the various pseudonyms used.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12673539457185785343" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1038129882" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1038129882" />
    <a title="Jean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16626474-jean"><img alt="Jean" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1038129882?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jean" href="/user/show/16626474-jean">Jean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1038129882" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14197937608034181030">I needed an antidote to 'Cousin Bette'. This seemed like a good 'bet'. Don't care for his column 'The Brother' or even 'The Plain People of Ireland,' but his columns 'The Research Bureau' with its many ingenious inventions, 'The Cathechism of Cliches', 'Keats and Chapman' are laugh-out-loud funny. His 'Binchy, Bergin, and Best' is included for Celticists like me who never knew the entire poem, or even who wrote it. O'Brian was also a well-informed locomotive enthusiast unless his hilarious colum</span>
  <span id="freeText14197937608034181030" style="display:none">I needed an antidote to 'Cousin Bette'. This seemed like a good 'bet'. Don't care for his column 'The Brother' or even 'The Plain People of Ireland,' but his columns 'The Research Bureau' with its many ingenious inventions, 'The Cathechism of Cliches', 'Keats and Chapman' are laugh-out-loud funny. His 'Binchy, Bergin, and Best' is included for Celticists like me who never knew the entire poem, or even who wrote it. O'Brian was also a well-informed locomotive enthusiast unless his hilarious columns are a cover-up for colossal ignorance. The man knew everything and could write on everything. Better read in small bites, one column per day, the better to digest his puns, general wordplay, irony, and unmatched cleverness, but if you read just one column per day of this 400 page volume, you would die first--so maybe three to four columns, and then you can die laughing. One example of what happens when a clever man like Myles takes a phrase literally: 'I found myself going homewards the other evening, not in a cab but in that odd mobile apartment with the dun-coloured wall-paper, a brown study. Long long thoughts occupied my mind'. A good slow read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14197937608034181030" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer928846266" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12572995408049136226">"Best of" is a bit of a misnomer, it is only the "Cruiskeen Lawn" columns from the first 5 years, through World War II. Some of this stuff is way too Irish, like, actually in Gaelic, or it seems like Gaelic and then when you try to pronounce it it turns out to be English. I got some real deep belly laughs out of this book though, funny stuff. I guess it is kind of important, because I have come across other Irish writings that casually mention "the Brother" or "the Plain People of Ireland" and I</span>
  <span id="freeText12572995408049136226" style="display:none">"Best of" is a bit of a misnomer, it is only the "Cruiskeen Lawn" columns from the first 5 years, through World War II. Some of this stuff is way too Irish, like, actually in Gaelic, or it seems like Gaelic and then when you try to pronounce it it turns out to be English. I got some real deep belly laughs out of this book though, funny stuff. I guess it is kind of important, because I have come across other Irish writings that casually mention "the Brother" or "the Plain People of Ireland" and I wouldn't have got those jokes if I hadn't read these. So it is funny, and it is also kind of a key into an inside Irish joke world. Mostly, though, just very funny and more than once I thought while reading it, "this guy is a genius." But I already knew that from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/97333.At_Swim_Two_Birds" title="At Swim-Two-Birds by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">At Swim-Two-Birds</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27208.The_Third_Policeman" title="The Third Policeman by Flann O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">The Third Policeman</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12572995408049136226" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15545049572572953596">A charming collection of column entries from one of my favourite Irish writers.   <span id="freeText15545049572572953596" style="display:none">A charming collection of column entries from one of my favourite Irish writers. The whole collection has the English-through-the-lens-of-Irish feel that characterises O'Brien's writing, with the exception of those few columns written in various flavours of Irish, which were charming, to the extent that I could follow them. <br /><br />At its maddest, it is stranger than The Third Policeman and At Swim Two Birds, and more light-hearted fun than An Béal Bocht. <br /><br />It's truly fantastic and a personal favourite, beating out some of the other collections (Myles Before Myles, Further Cuttings, and Myles from Dublin), though they set a high bar.</span>

This means that everyone of his "Keats" pieces, which are giant set ups for groaner puns it laid end-to-end so no matter how good the individual pieces are, you're sick of them long before you get to the end of the chapter.<br/><br/>Still a fine bathroom book.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer362443198" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3823377266039422906">If this is the same edition as my Best of Myles, then this is endless, boundless, innovative genius. If it's not, then it will be something else, but potentially also seeded with ingenuity and lunacy beyond all decent repair. In a recent bout of anniversarial nostalgia, The Irish Times printed a selection of vintage Myles columns as tribute. They were still easily the most vibrant, fluent and relevant pieces in the newspaper, despite being sixty years old. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_26603782" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26603782" />
    <a title="Leonard Pierce" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/377317-leonard-pierce"><img alt="Leonard Pierce" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451717588p2/377317.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26603782?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leonard Pierce" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leonard Pierce" href="/user/show/377317-leonard-pierce">Leonard Pierce</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/377317-leonard-pierce?shelf=humor">humor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26603782" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3874984574282382388">Simply the best collection available of one of the funniest writers in the English (and, for that matter, Irish) language. A number of the pieces in this are amongst the funniest, most skillful, and elegant humor pieces I've ever read, and there's some to-rate criticism in here, too.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('pt2Uph+ZAKGkcjrEmgIFP0koK22o0S7aUCS9jQDoPllxyvbIt7xQ4IOmJf3iZFHxBIuzslv97/3dMHSyEnDewg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Tides, Nick’s second book, is the much-anticipated follow-up to his critically acclaimed debut Echoes.Now retired from the strain of work as a prison officer, Nick is free to climb. A lot. Tides is a treasury of his antics and adventures with some of the world’s leading climbers, including Steve House, Kenton Cool, Nico Favresse, Andy Houseman and James McHaffie. Follow Nick and his partners as they push the limits on some of the world’s most serious routes: The Bells! The Bells! on Gogarth’s North Stack Wall; the Slovak Direct on Denali; Guerdon Grooves on Buachaille Etive Mor; and the north faces of Chang Himal and Mount Alberta, among countless others.Nick’s life can be equated to the rhythm of the sea. At high tide, he climbs, he loves it, he is good at it; he laughs and jokes, scares himself, falls, gets back up and climbs some more. Then the tide goes out and he finds himself alone, exposed, all questions and no answers. Self-doubt, grieving for friends or family, fearful, sometimes opinionated, occasionally angry – his writing more honest and exposed than in any account of a climb. Only when the tide turns is he able to forget once more.Tides is a gripping memoir that captures the very essence of what it means to dedicate one’s life to climbing.<\/span>\n  <a
